<p><b>Japanese</b><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">日本語</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><i>Nihongo</i></i></span>, <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="ja-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Japanese" title="Help:IPA/Japanese">[ɲihoŋɡo]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ja-nihongo.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/99/Ja-nihongo.ogg" title="Ja-nihongo.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>)</span> is spoken natively by about 128 million people, primarily by <a href="/wiki/Japanese_people" title="Japanese people">Japanese people</a> and primarily in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, the only country where it is the <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">national language</a>. Japanese belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Japonic_languages" title="Japonic languages">Japonic</a> or Japanese-<a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_languages" title="Ryukyuan languages">Ryukyuan</a> language family. There have been many <a href="/wiki/Classification_of_the_Japonic_languages" title="Classification of the Japonic languages">attempts to group the Japonic languages</a> with other families such as the <a href="/wiki/Ainu_languages" title="Ainu languages">Ainu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austroasiatic_languages" title="Austroasiatic languages">Austroasiatic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koreanic_languages" title="Koreanic languages">Koreanic</a>, and the now-discredited <a href="/wiki/Altaic_languages" title="Altaic languages">Altaic</a>, but none of these proposals has gained widespread acceptance.
</p><p>Little is known of the language's prehistory, or when it first appeared in Japan. Chinese documents from the 3rd century AD recorded a few Japanese words, but substantial <a href="/wiki/Old_Japanese" title="Old Japanese">Old Japanese</a> texts did not appear until the 8th century. From the <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Heian period</a> (794–1185), there was a massive influx of <a href="/wiki/Sino-Japanese_vocabulary" title="Sino-Japanese vocabulary">Sino-Japanese vocabulary</a> into the language, affecting the <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a> of <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Japanese" title="Early Middle Japanese">Early Middle Japanese</a>. <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Japanese" title="Late Middle Japanese">Late Middle Japanese</a> (1185–1600) saw extensive grammatical changes and the first appearance of <a href="/wiki/Gairaigo" title="Gairaigo">European loanwords</a>. The basis of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Japanese" title="Standard Japanese">standard dialect</a> moved from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kansai" title="Kansai">Kansai</a> region to the <a href="/wiki/Edo" title="Edo">Edo</a> region (modern <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Japanese" title="Early Modern Japanese">Early Modern Japanese</a> period (early 17th century–mid 19th century). Following the end of <a href="/wiki/Sakoku" title="Sakoku">Japan's self-imposed isolation</a> in 1853, the flow of <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a> from European languages increased significantly, and <a href="/wiki/Wasei-eigo" title="Wasei-eigo">words from English roots</a> have proliferated.
</p><p>Japanese is an <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">agglutinative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mora_(linguistics)#Japanese" title="Mora (linguistics)">mora</a>-timed language with relatively simple <a href="/wiki/Phonotactics" title="Phonotactics">phonotactics</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">pure vowel</a> system, <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length#Phonemic_vowel_length" title="Vowel length">phonemic vowel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">consonant</a> length, and a lexically significant <a href="/wiki/Japanese_pitch_accent" title="Japanese pitch accent">pitch-accent</a>. Word order is normally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" title="Subject–object–verb">subject–object–verb</a> with <a href="/wiki/Japanese_particles" title="Japanese particles">particles</a> marking the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammatical_function" title="Grammatical function">grammatical function</a> of words, and sentence structure is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topic%E2%80%93comment" title="Topic–comment">topic–comment</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sentence-final_particle" title="Sentence-final particle">Sentence-final particles</a> are used to add emotional or emphatic impact, or form questions. Nouns have no <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">grammatical number</a> or <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a>, and there are no <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">articles</a>. Verbs are <a href="/wiki/Japanese_verb_conjugation" title="Japanese verb conjugation">conjugated</a>, primarily for <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tense</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voice_(grammar)" title="Voice (grammar)">voice</a>, but not <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">person</a>. <a href="/wiki/Japanese_adjectives" title="Japanese adjectives">Japanese adjectives</a> are also conjugated. Japanese has <a href="/wiki/Honorific_speech_in_Japanese" title="Honorific speech in Japanese">a complex system of honorifics</a>, with verb forms and vocabulary to indicate the relative status of the speaker, the listener, and persons mentioned.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Japanese_writing_system" title="Japanese writing system">Japanese writing system</a> combines <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>, known as <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">漢字</span></span>, '<a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a> characters')</span>, with two unique <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabaries</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Mora_(linguistics)" title="Mora (linguistics)">moraic</a> scripts) derived by the Japanese from the more complex Chinese characters: <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a></i></span> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ひらがな</span></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">平仮名</span></span>, 'simple characters') and <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a></i></span> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">カタカナ</span></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">片仮名</span></span>, 'partial characters'). <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> (<span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">rōmaji</i></span> <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ローマ字</span></span>) is also used in a limited fashion (such as for imported acronyms) in Japanese writing. The <a href="/wiki/Japanese_numerals" title="Japanese numerals">numeral system</a> uses mostly <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">Arabic numerals</a>, but also traditional <a href="/wiki/Chinese_numerals" title="Chinese numerals">Chinese numerals</a>.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_writing_system#History_of_the_Japanese_script" title="Japanese writing system">Japanese writing system § History of the Japanese script</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Proto-Japonic" title="Proto-Japonic">Proto-Japonic</a>, the common ancestor of the Japanese and <a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_languages" title="Ryukyuan languages">Ryukyuan languages</a>, is thought to have been brought to Japan by settlers coming from the Korean peninsula sometime in the early- to mid-4th century BC (the <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi period</a>), replacing the languages of the original <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_people" title="Jōmon people">Jōmon</a> inhabitants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> including the ancestor of the modern <a href="/wiki/Ainu_language" title="Ainu language">Ainu language</a>. Because writing had yet to be introduced from China, there is no direct evidence, and anything that can be discerned about this period must be based on internal reconstruction from <a href="/wiki/Old_Japanese" title="Old Japanese">Old Japanese</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Comparative_method" title="Comparative method">comparison</a> with the Ryukyuan languages and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_dialects" title="Japanese dialects">Japanese dialects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvigWhitman2008[httpsbooksgooglecoukbooksidaun8BRHTDEAClpgPA1_1]_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvigWhitman2008[httpsbooksgooglecoukbooksidaun8BRHTDEAClpgPA1_1]-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Japanese">Old Japanese</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Genryaku_Manyosyu.JPG"><img alt="Page from the Man'yōshū" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1333" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Genryaku_Manyosyu.JPG/220px-Genryaku_Manyosyu.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Genryaku_Manyosyu.JPG/330px-Genryaku_Manyosyu.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Genryaku_Manyosyu.JPG/440px-Genryaku_Manyosyu.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Genryaku_Manyosyu.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the <i><a href="/wiki/Man%27y%C5%8Dsh%C5%AB" title="Man'yōshū">Man'yōshū</a></i>, the oldest anthology of classical <a href="/wiki/Japanese_poetry" title="Japanese poetry">Japanese poetry</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Japanese" title="Old Japanese">Old Japanese</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese writing system</a> was imported to Japan from <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a> around the start of the fifth century, alongside Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201011_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201011-4">[4]</a></sup> The earliest texts were written in <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a>, although some of these were likely intended to be read as Japanese using the <a href="/wiki/Kanbun" title="Kanbun">kanbun</a> method, and show influences of Japanese grammar such as Japanese word order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeeley199125–31_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeeley199125–31-5">[5]</a></sup> The earliest text, the <i><a href="/wiki/Kojiki" title="Kojiki">Kojiki</a></i>, dates to the early eighth century, and was written entirely in Chinese characters, which are used to represent, at different times, Chinese, <i>kanbun</i>, and Old Japanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201024_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201024-6">[6]</a></sup> As in other texts from this period, the Old Japanese sections are written in <a href="/wiki/Man%27y%C5%8Dgana" title="Man'yōgana">Man'yōgana</a>, which uses <i><a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a></i> for their phonetic as well as semantic values.
</p><p>Based on the Man'yōgana system, Old Japanese can be reconstructed as having 88 distinct syllables. Texts written with Man'yōgana use two different sets of <i>kanji</i> for each of the syllables now pronounced <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">き</span></span> (ki), <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ひ</span></span> (hi), <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">み</span></span> (mi), <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">け</span></span> (ke), <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">へ</span></span> (he), <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">め</span></span> (me), <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">こ</span></span> (ko), <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">そ</span></span> (so), <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">と</span></span> (to), <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">の</span></span> (no), <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">も</span></span> (mo), <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">よ</span></span> (yo) and <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ろ</span></span> (ro).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> (The <i>Kojiki</i> has 88, but all later texts have 87. The distinction between mo<sub>1</sub> and mo<sub>2</sub> apparently was lost immediately following its composition.) This set of syllables shrank to 67 in <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Japanese" title="Early Middle Japanese">Early Middle Japanese</a>, though some were added through Chinese influence. Man'yōgana also has a symbol for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/je/</span>, which merges with <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span> before the end of the period.
</p><p>Several fossilizations of Old Japanese grammatical elements remain in the modern language – the genitive particle <i>tsu</i> (superseded by modern <i>no</i>) is preserved in words such as <i>matsuge</i> ("eyelash", lit. "hair of the eye"); modern <i>mieru</i> ("to be visible") and <i>kikoeru</i> ("to be audible") retain a <a href="/wiki/Mediopassive_voice" title="Mediopassive voice">mediopassive</a> suffix -<i>yu(ru)</i> (<i>kikoyu</i> → <i>kikoyuru</i> (the attributive form, which slowly replaced the plain form starting in the late Heian period) → <i>kikoeru</i> (all verbs with the <i>shimo-nidan</i> conjugation pattern underwent this same shift in <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Japanese" title="Early Modern Japanese">Early Modern Japanese</a>)); and the genitive particle <i>ga</i> remains in intentionally archaic speech.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Japanese">Early Middle Japanese</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg"><img alt="Genji Monogatari emaki scroll" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="2142" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/220px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/330px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/440px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 12th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emaki" title="Emaki">emaki</a> scroll of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i> from the 11th century</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Japanese" title="Early Middle Japanese">Early Middle Japanese</a></div>
<p>Early Middle Japanese is the Japanese of the <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Heian period</a>, from 794 to 1185. It formed the basis for the <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary standard</a> of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Japanese" title="Classical Japanese">Classical Japanese</a>, which remained in common use until the early 20th century.
</p><p>During this time, Japanese underwent numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonological" title="Phonological">phonological</a> developments, in many cases instigated by an influx of <a href="/wiki/Sino-Japanese_vocabulary" title="Sino-Japanese vocabulary">Chinese loanwords</a>. These included phonemic length distinction for both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geminate_consonant" title="Geminate consonant">consonants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">vowels</a>, palatal consonants (e.g. <i>kya</i>) and labial consonant clusters (e.g. <i>kwa</i>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed_syllable" title="Closed syllable">closed syllables</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frel_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frel-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> This had the effect of changing Japanese into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mora-timed" title="Mora-timed">mora-timed</a> language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frel_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frel-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Middle_Japanese">Late Middle Japanese</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Japanese" title="Late Middle Japanese">Late Middle Japanese</a></div>
<p>Late Middle Japanese covers the years from 1185 to 1600, and is normally divided into two sections, roughly equivalent to the <a href="/wiki/Kamakura_period" title="Kamakura period">Kamakura period</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Muromachi_period" title="Muromachi period">Muromachi period</a>, respectively. The later forms of Late Middle Japanese are the first to be described by non-native sources, in this case the <a href="/wiki/Jesuits" title="Jesuits">Jesuit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franciscans" title="Franciscans">Franciscan</a> missionaries; and thus there is better documentation of Late Middle Japanese phonology than for previous forms (for instance, the <i><a href="/wiki/Arte_da_Lingoa_de_Iapam" title="Arte da Lingoa de Iapam">Arte da Lingoa de Iapam</a></i>). Among other sound changes, the sequence <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/au/</span> merges to <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɔː/</span>, in contrast with <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/oː/</span>; <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/p/</span> is reintroduced from Chinese; and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/we/</span> merges with <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/je/</span>. Some forms rather more familiar to Modern Japanese speakers begin to appear – the continuative ending -<i>te</i> begins to reduce onto the verb (e.g. <i>yonde</i> for earlier <i>yomite</i>), the -k- in the final syllable of adjectives drops out (<i>shiroi</i> for earlier <i>shiroki</i>); and some forms exist where modern standard Japanese has retained the earlier form (e.g. <i>hayaku</i> &gt; <i>hayau</i> &gt; <i>hayɔɔ</i>, where modern Japanese just has <i>hayaku</i>, though the alternative form is preserved in the standard greeting <i>o-hayō gozaimasu</i> "good morning"; this ending is also seen in <i>o-medetō</i> "congratulations", from <i>medetaku</i>).
</p><p>Late Middle Japanese has the first loanwords from European languages – now-common words borrowed into Japanese in this period include <i>pan</i> ("bread") and <i>tabako</i> ("tobacco", now "cigarette"), both from <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Japanese">Modern Japanese</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Standard Japanese" redirects here. For other dialects, see <a href="/wiki/Japanese_dialects" title="Japanese dialects">Japanese dialects</a>.</div>
<p>Modern Japanese is considered to begin with the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> (which spanned from 1603 to 1867). Since Old Japanese, the <i>de facto</i> standard Japanese had been the <a href="/wiki/Kansai_dialect" title="Kansai dialect">Kansai dialect</a>, especially that of <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>. However, during the Edo period, Edo (now Tokyo) developed into the largest city in Japan, and the Edo-area dialect became standard Japanese. Since the end of <a href="/wiki/Sakoku" title="Sakoku">Japan's self-imposed isolation</a> in 1853, the flow of loanwords from European languages has increased significantly. The period since 1945 has seen many words borrowed from other languages—such as German, Portuguese and English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Many English loan words especially relate to technology—for example, <i>pasokon</i> (short for "personal computer"), <i>intānetto</i> ("internet"), and <i>kamera</i> ("camera"). Due to the large quantity of English loanwords, modern Japanese has developed a distinction between <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[tɕi]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ti]</span>, and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[dʑi]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[di]</span>, with the latter in each pair only found in loanwords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span></h2>
<p>Although Japanese is spoken almost exclusively in Japan, it has been spoken outside. Before and during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, through Japanese annexation of <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, as well as partial occupation of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, and various Pacific islands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> locals in <a href="/wiki/Greater_East_Asia_Co-Prosperity_Sphere" title="Greater East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere">those countries</a> learned Japanese as the language of the empire. As a result, many elderly people in these countries can still speak Japanese.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Japanese_diaspora" title="Japanese diaspora">Japanese emigrant communities</a> (the largest of which are to be found in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IBGE_traça_perfil_dos_imigrantes_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-IBGE_traça_perfil_dos_imigrantes-13">[13]</a></sup> with 1.4 million to 1.5 million Japanese immigrants and descendants, according to Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Institute_of_Geography_and_Statistics" title="Brazilian Institute of Geography and Statistics">IBGE</a> data, more than the 1.2 million of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>) sometimes employ Japanese as their primary language. Approximately 12% of <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> residents speak Japanese,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> with an estimated 12.6% of the population of Japanese ancestry in 2008. Japanese emigrants can also be found in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> (especially in the eastern states), <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> (especially in <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a> where 1.4% of the population has Japanese ancestry<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>), the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (notably <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, where 16.7% of the population has Japanese ancestry<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="above it says 12.6%; which is it? (September 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> (particularly in <a href="/wiki/Davao_Region" title="Davao Region">Davao region</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laguna_(province)" title="Laguna (province)">Laguna</a> province).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">Official status</span></h3>
<p>Japanese has no <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official status</a> in Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> but is the <i>de facto</i> <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">national language</a> of the country. There is a form of the language considered standard: <i>hyōjungo</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">標準語</span></span>)</span>, meaning "standard Japanese", or <i>kyōtsūgo</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">共通語</span></span>)</span>, "common language". The meanings of the two terms are almost the same. <i>Hyōjungo</i> or <i>kyōtsūgo</i> is a conception that forms the counterpart of dialect. This normative language was born after the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">明治維新</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">meiji ishin</i></span>, 1868)</span> from the language spoken in the higher-class areas of Tokyo (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yamanote" title="Yamanote">Yamanote</a>). <i>Hyōjungo</i> is taught in schools and used on television and in official communications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> It is the version of Japanese discussed in this article.
</p><p>Formerly, standard Japanese in writing<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">文語</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bungo_(Japanese_language)" title="Bungo (Japanese language)">bungo</a></i></span>, "literary language")</span> was different from colloquial language<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">口語</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kogo_(Japanese_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kogo (Japanese language) (page does not exist)">kōgo</a></i></span>)</span>. The two systems have different rules of grammar and some variance in vocabulary. <i>Bungo</i> was the main method of writing Japanese until about 1900; since then <i>kōgo</i> gradually extended its influence and the two methods were both used in writing until the 1940s. <i>Bungo</i> still has some relevance for historians, literary scholars, and lawyers (many Japanese laws that survived <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> are still written in <i>bungo</i>, although there are ongoing efforts to modernize their language). <i>Kōgo</i> is the dominant method of both speaking and writing Japanese today, although <i>bungo</i> grammar and vocabulary are occasionally used in modern Japanese for effect.
</p><p>The 1982 state constitution of <a href="/wiki/Angaur" title="Angaur">Angaur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palau" title="Palau">Palau</a>, names Japanese along with <a href="/wiki/Palauan_language" title="Palauan language">Palauan</a> and English as an official language of the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-const_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-const-23">[23]</a></sup> However, the results of the 2005 census show that in April 2005 there were no usual or legal residents of Angaur aged 5 or older who spoke Japanese at home at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005census_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-2005census-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects_and_mutual_intelligibility">Dialects and mutual intelligibility</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_dialects" title="Japanese dialects">Japanese dialects</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Japanese_dialects-en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1905" data-file-width="2030" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Japanese_dialects-en.png/220px-Japanese_dialects-en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Japanese_dialects-en.png/330px-Japanese_dialects-en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Japanese_dialects-en.png/440px-Japanese_dialects-en.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Japanese_dialects-en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Japanese dialects and Japonic languages</div></div></div>
<p>Japanese dialects typically differ in terms of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_pitch_accent" title="Japanese pitch accent">pitch accent</a>, inflectional <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a>, and particle usage. Some even differ in <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonant</a> inventories, although this is uncommon.
</p><p>In terms of <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutual intelligibility</a>, a survey in 1967 found the four most unintelligible dialects (excluding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ry%C5%ABky%C5%ABan_languages" title="Ryūkyūan languages">Ryūkyūan languages</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tohoku_dialects" title="Tohoku dialects">Tohoku dialects</a>) to students from Greater Tokyo are the <a href="/wiki/Kiso,_Nagano_(town)" title="Kiso, Nagano (town)">Kiso</a> dialect (in the deep mountains of <a href="/wiki/Nagano_Prefecture" title="Nagano Prefecture">Nagano Prefecture</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Himi,_Toyama" title="Himi, Toyama">Himi</a> dialect (in <a href="/wiki/Toyama_Prefecture" title="Toyama Prefecture">Toyama Prefecture</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Kagoshima_dialect" title="Kagoshima dialect">Kagoshima dialect</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Katsuyama,_Okayama" title="Katsuyama, Okayama">Maniwa</a> dialect (in <a href="/wiki/Okayama_Prefecture" title="Okayama Prefecture">Okayama Prefecture</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dialect_Intelligibility_1967_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dialect_Intelligibility_1967-25">[25]</a></sup> The survey is based on recordings of 12- to 20- second long, of 135 to 244 <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a>, which 42 students listened and translated word-by-word. The listeners are all <a href="/wiki/Keio_University" title="Keio University">Keio University</a> students who grew up in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanto_region" title="Kanto region">Kanto region</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dialect_Intelligibility_1967_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dialect_Intelligibility_1967-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable sortable">

<p>There are some <a href="/wiki/Language_island" title="Language island">language islands</a> in mountain villages or isolated islands such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hachij%C5%8Djima" title="Hachijōjima">Hachijō-jima island</a> whose dialects are descended from the Eastern dialect of <a href="/wiki/Old_Japanese" title="Old Japanese">Old Japanese</a>. Dialects of the <a href="/wiki/Kansai_region" title="Kansai region">Kansai region</a> are spoken or known by many Japanese, and <a href="/wiki/Osaka" title="Osaka">Osaka</a> dialect in particular is associated with comedy (see <a href="/wiki/Kansai_dialect" title="Kansai dialect">Kansai dialect</a>). Dialects of Tōhoku and North <a href="/wiki/Kant%C5%8D_region" title="Kantō region">Kantō</a> are associated with typical farmers.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ry%C5%ABky%C5%ABan_languages" title="Ryūkyūan languages">Ryūkyūan languages</a>, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Prefecture" title="Okinawa Prefecture">Okinawa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Amami_Islands" title="Amami Islands">Amami Islands</a> (politically part of <a href="/wiki/Kagoshima_Prefecture" title="Kagoshima Prefecture">Kagoshima</a>), are distinct enough to be considered a separate branch of the <a href="/wiki/Japonic_languages" title="Japonic languages">Japonic</a> family; not only is each language unintelligible to Japanese speakers, but most are unintelligible to those who speak other Ryūkyūan languages. However, in contrast to linguists, many ordinary Japanese people tend to consider the Ryūkyūan languages as dialects of Japanese. The imperial court also seems to have spoken an unusual variant of the Japanese of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Most likely being the spoken form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Japanese_language" title="Classical Japanese language">Classical Japanese language</a>, a writing style that was prevalent during the <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Heian period</a>, but began decline during the late <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiji_period" title="Meiji period">Meiji period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ry%C5%ABky%C5%ABan_languages" title="Ryūkyūan languages">Ryūkyūan languages</a> are spoken by a decreasing number of elderly people so <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> classified it as endangered, because they could become extinct by 2050. Young people mostly use Japanese and cannot understand the Ryukyuan languages. <a href="/wiki/Okinawan_Japanese" title="Okinawan Japanese">Okinawan Japanese</a> is a variant of Standard Japanese influenced by the Ryukyuan languages. It is the primary dialect spoken among young people in the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ryukyuan-tongue_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-ryukyuan-tongue-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern Japanese has become prevalent nationwide (including the Ryūkyū islands) due to <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">mass media</a>, and an increase of mobility within Japan, as well as economic integration.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classification_of_the_Japonic_languages" title="Classification of the Japonic languages">Classification of the Japonic languages</a></div>
<p>Japanese is a member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japonic_language" title="Japonic language">Japonic language</a> family, which also includes the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_languages" title="Ryukyuan languages">Ryukyuan languages</a> spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a>. As these closely related languages are commonly treated as dialects of the same language, Japanese is often called a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martine_Irma_Robbeets" title="Martine Irma Robbeets">Martine Irma Robbeets</a>, Japanese has been subject to more attempts to show its relation to other languages than any other language in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobbeets200520_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobbeets200520-30">[30]</a></sup> Since Japanese first gained the consideration of linguists in the late 19th century, attempts have been made to show its genealogical relation to languages or language families such as <a href="/wiki/Ainu_languages" title="Ainu languages">Ainu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tibeto-Burman_languages" title="Tibeto-Burman languages">Tibeto-Burman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Altaic_languages" title="Altaic languages">Altaic</a> (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ural%E2%80%93Altaic_languages" title="Ural–Altaic languages">Ural-Altaic</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mon%E2%80%93Khmer_languages" title="Mon–Khmer languages">Mon–Khmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malayo-Polynesian_languages" title="Malayo-Polynesian languages">Malayo-Polynesian</a>. At the fringe, some linguists have suggested a link to <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, and to <a href="/wiki/Lepcha_language" title="Lepcha language">Lepcha</a>. Main modern theories try to link Japanese either to northern Asian languages, like Korean or the proposed larger Altaic family, or to various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southeast_Asian_language" title="Southeast Asian language">Southeast Asian languages</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian</a>. None of these proposals have gained wide acceptance (and the Altaic family itself is now considered controversial).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobbeets2005_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobbeets2005-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2010_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2010-33">[33]</a></sup> As it stands, only the link to Ryukyuan has wide support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKindaichiHirano197830–31_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKindaichiHirano197830–31-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other theories view the Japanese language as an early <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">creole language</a> formed through inputs from at least two distinct language groups, or as a distinct language of its own that has absorbed various aspects from neighbouring languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_phonology" title="Japanese phonology">Japanese phonology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><audio class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="99" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Ja-botchan_1-1_1-2.ogg" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="56135" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (56 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/26/Ja-botchan_1-1_1-2.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="97728" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/26/Ja-botchan_1-1_1-2.ogg/Ja-botchan_1-1_1-2.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ja-botchan_1-1_1-2.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spoken Japanese</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Japanese_vowel_chart_II.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="980" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Japanese_vowel_chart_II.svg/250px-Japanese_vowel_chart_II.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Japanese_vowel_chart_II.svg/375px-Japanese_vowel_chart_II.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Japanese_vowel_chart_II.svg/500px-Japanese_vowel_chart_II.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Japanese_vowel_chart_II.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The vowels of Standard Japanese on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vowel_chart" title="Vowel chart">vowel chart</a>. </th>
<td></td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">a</a></span></td>
<td>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Japanese has five vowels, and <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">vowel length</a> is phonemic, with each having both a short and a long version. Elongated vowels are usually denoted with a line over the vowel (a <a href="/wiki/Macron_(diacritic)" title="Macron (diacritic)">macron</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Japanese" title="Romanization of Japanese">rōmaji</a>, a repeated vowel character in <a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a>, or a <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Donpu" title="Chōonpu">chōonpu</a> succeeding the vowel in <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a>. <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span> <span style="font-size:85%;">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ja-U.oga" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Ja-U.oga" title="Ja-U.oga">listen</a></span></span>)</span> is <a href="/wiki/Roundedness" title="Roundedness">compressed rather than protruded</a>, or simply unrounded.
</p>

<tr>
<th>Special moras
</th>
<td colspan="7"><a href="#Moraic_nasal"><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/N/</span></a>, <a href="#Gemination"><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/Q/</span></a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Some Japanese consonants have several <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophones</a>, which may give the impression of a larger inventory of sounds. However, some of these allophones have since become phonemic. For example, in the Japanese language up to and including the first half of the 20th century, the phonemic sequence <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ti/</span> was <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">palatalized</a> and realized phonetically as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[tɕi]</span>, approximately <i>chi</i> <span style="font-size:85%;">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ja-Chi_2.oga" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5a/Ja-Chi_2.oga" title="Ja-Chi 2.oga">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; however, now <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ti]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[tɕi]</span> are distinct, as evidenced by words like <i>tī</i> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[tiː]</span> "Western-style tea" and <i>chii</i> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[tɕii]</span> "social status".
</p><p>The "r" of the Japanese language is of particular interest, ranging between an <a href="/wiki/Apical_consonant" title="Apical consonant">apical</a> <a href="/wiki/Central_consonant" title="Central consonant">central</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tap_consonant" title="Tap consonant">tap</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lateral_approximant" title="Lateral approximant">lateral approximant</a>. The "g" is also notable; unless it starts a sentence, it may be pronounced <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_nasal" title="Voiced velar nasal">ŋ</a>]</span>, in the Kanto prestige dialect and in other eastern dialects.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Phonotactics" title="Phonotactics">phonotactics</a> of Japanese are relatively simple. The syllable structure is (C)(G)V(C),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> that is, a core vowel surrounded by an optional onset consonant, a glide <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span> and either the first part of a <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">geminate consonant</a> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">っ</span></span></span>/<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ッ</span></span></span>, represented as Q) or a <a href="/wiki/Japanese_phonology#Moraic_nasal" title="Japanese phonology">moraic nasal</a> in the coda (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ん</span></span></span>/<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ン</span></span></span>, represented as N).
</p><p>The nasal is sensitive to its phonetic environment and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">assimilates</a> to the following phoneme, with pronunciations including <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɴ, m, n, ɲ, ŋ, ɰ̃]</span>. Onset-glide clusters only occur at the start of syllables but clusters across syllables are allowed as long as the two consonants are the moraic nasal followed by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homorganic" title="Homorganic">homorganic</a> consonant.
</p><p>Japanese also includes a <a href="/wiki/Japanese_pitch_accent" title="Japanese pitch accent">pitch accent</a>, which is not represented in syllabic writing; for example <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[haꜜ.ɕi]</span> ("chopsticks") and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ha.ɕiꜜ]</span> ("bridge") are both spelled <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">はし</span></span></span> (<i>hashi</i>), and are only differentiated by the tone contour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_grammar" title="Japanese grammar">Japanese grammar</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output
