<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sentence_structure">Sentence structure</span></h3>
<p>Japanese word order is classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" title="Subject–object–verb">subject–object–verb</a>. Unlike many <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>, the only strict rule of word order is that the verb must be placed at the end of a sentence (possibly followed by sentence-end particles). This is because Japanese sentence elements are marked with <a href="/wiki/Japanese_particles" title="Japanese particles">particles</a> that identify their grammatical functions.
</p><p>The basic sentence structure is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topic%E2%80%93comment" title="Topic–comment">topic–comment</a>. For example, <i>Kochira wa Tanaka-san desu</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">こちらは田中さんです</span></span></span>). <i>kochira</i> ("this") is the topic of the sentence, indicated by the particle <i>wa</i>. The verb <i>de aru</i> (<i>desu</i> is a contraction of its polite form <i>de arimasu</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">copula</a>, commonly translated as "to be" or "it is" (though there are other verbs that can be translated as "to be"), though technically it holds no meaning and is used to give a sentence 'politeness'. As a phrase, <i>Tanaka-san desu</i> is the comment. This sentence literally translates to "As for this person, (it) is Mx Tanaka." Thus Japanese, like many other Asian languages, is often called a <a href="/wiki/Topic-prominent_language" title="Topic-prominent language">topic-prominent language</a>, which means it has a strong tendency to indicate the topic separately from the subject, and that the two do not always coincide. The sentence <i>Zō wa hana ga nagai</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">象は鼻が長い</span></span></span>) literally means, "As for elephant(s), (the) nose(s) (is/are) long". The topic is <i>zō</i> "elephant", and the subject is <i>hana</i> "nose".
</p><p>In Japanese, the subject or object of a sentence need not be stated if it is obvious from context. As a result of this grammatical permissiveness, there is a tendency to gravitate towards brevity; Japanese speakers tend to omit <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronouns</a> on the theory they are inferred from the previous sentence, and are therefore understood. In the context of the above example, <i>hana-ga nagai</i> would mean "[their] noses are long," while <i>nagai</i> by itself would mean "[they] are long." A single verb can be a complete sentence: <i>Yatta!</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">やった!</span></span></span>) "[I / we / they / etc] did [it]!". In addition, since adjectives can form the predicate in a Japanese sentence (below), a single adjective can be a complete sentence: <i>Urayamashii!</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">羨ましい!</span></span></span>) "[I'm] jealous [of it]!".
</p><p>While the language has some words that are typically translated as pronouns, these are not used as frequently as pronouns in some Indo-European languages, and function differently. In some cases Japanese relies on special verb forms and auxiliary verbs to indicate the direction of benefit of an action: "down" to indicate the out-group gives a benefit to the in-group; and "up" to indicate the in-group gives a benefit to the out-group. Here, the in-group includes the speaker and the out-group does not, and their boundary depends on context. For example, <i>oshiete moratta</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">教えてもらった</span></span></span>) (literally, "explained" with a benefit from the out-group to the in-group) means "[he/she/they] explained [it] to [me/us]". Similarly, <i>oshiete ageta</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">教えてあげた</span></span></span>) (literally, "explained" with a benefit from the in-group to the out-group) means "[I/we] explained [it] to [him/her/them]". Such beneficiary auxiliary verbs thus serve a function comparable to that of pronouns and prepositions in Indo-European languages to indicate the actor and the recipient of an action.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Japanese_pronouns" title="Japanese pronouns">Japanese "pronouns"</a> also function differently from most modern Indo-European pronouns (and more like nouns) in that they can take modifiers as any other noun may. For instance, one does not say in English:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The amazed he ran down the street. (grammatically incorrect insertion of a pronoun)</p></blockquote>
<p>But one <i>can</i> grammatically say essentially the same thing in Japanese:
</p>
<blockquote><div class="poem">
<p><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">驚いた彼は道を走っていった。</span></span></span><br/>
Transliteration: <i>Odoroita kare wa michi o hashitte itta.</i> (grammatically correct)
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<p>This is partly because these words evolved from regular nouns, such as <i>kimi</i> "you" (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">君</span></span></span> "lord"), <i>anata</i> "you" (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">あなた</span></span></span> "that side, yonder"), and <i>boku</i> "I" (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">僕</span></span></span> "servant"). This is why some linguists do not classify Japanese "pronouns" as pronouns, but rather as referential nouns, much like Spanish <i>usted</i> (contracted from <i>vuestra merced</i>, "your [(flattering <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majestic_plural" title="Majestic plural">majestic</a>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majestic_plural" title="Majestic plural">plural</a>] grace") or Portuguese <i>o senhor</i>. Japanese personal pronouns are generally used only in situations requiring special emphasis as to who is doing what to whom.
</p><p>The choice of words used as pronouns is correlated with the sex of the speaker and the social situation in which they are spoken: men and women alike in a formal situation generally refer to themselves as <i>watashi</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">私</span></span></span> "private") or <i>watakushi</i> (also <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">私</span></span></span>), while men in rougher or intimate conversation are much more likely to use the word <i>ore</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">俺</span></span></span> "oneself", "myself") or <i>boku</i>. Similarly, different words such as <i>anata</i>, <i>kimi</i>, and <i>omae</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">お前</span></span></span>, more formally <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">御前</span></span></span> "the one before me") may refer to a listener depending on the listener's relative social position and the degree of familiarity between the speaker and the listener. When used in different social relationships, the same word may have positive (intimate or respectful) or negative (distant or disrespectful) connotations.
</p><p>Japanese often use titles of the person referred to where pronouns would be used in English. For example, when speaking to one's teacher, it is appropriate to use <i>sensei</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">先生</span></span></span>, teacher), but inappropriate to use <i>anata</i>. This is because <i>anata</i> is used to refer to people of equal or lower status, and one's teacher has higher status.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inflection_and_conjugation">Inflection and conjugation</span></h3>
<p>Japanese nouns have no grammatical number, gender or article aspect. The noun <i>hon</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">本</span></span></span>) may refer to a single book or several books; <i>hito</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">人</span></span></span>) can mean "person" or "people", and <i>ki</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">木</span></span></span>) can be "tree" or "trees". Where number is important, it can be indicated by providing a quantity (often with a <a href="/wiki/Japanese_counter_word" title="Japanese counter word">counter word</a>) or (rarely) by adding a suffix,  or sometimes by duplication (e.g. <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">人人</span></span></span>, <i>hitobito</i>, usually written with an iteration mark as <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">人々</span></span></span>). Words for people are usually understood as singular. Thus <i>Tanaka-san</i> usually means <i>Mx Tanaka</i>. Words that refer to people and animals can be made to indicate a group of individuals through the addition of a collective suffix (a noun suffix that indicates a group), such as <i>-tachi</i>, but this is not a true plural: the meaning is closer to the English phrase "and company". A group described as <i>Tanaka-san-tachi</i> may include people not named Tanaka. Some Japanese nouns are effectively plural, such as <i>hitobito</i> "people" and <i>wareware</i> "we/us", while the word <i>tomodachi</i> "friend" is considered singular, although plural in form.
</p><p>Verbs are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_verb_conjugations" title="Japanese verb conjugations">conjugated</a> to show tenses, of which there are two: past and present (or non-past) which is used for the present and the future. For verbs that represent an ongoing process, the <i>-te iru</i> form indicates a continuous (or progressive) <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspect</a>, similar to the suffix <i>ing</i> in English. For others that represent a change of state, the <i>-te iru</i> form indicates a perfect aspect. For example, <i>kite iru</i> means "They have come (and are still here)", but <i>tabete iru</i> means "They are eating".
</p><p>Questions (both with an interrogative pronoun and yes/no questions) have the same structure as affirmative sentences, but with intonation rising at the end. In the formal register, the question particle <i>-ka</i> is added. For example, <i>ii desu</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">いいです</span></span></span>) "It is OK" becomes <i>ii desu-ka</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">いいですか。</span></span></span>) "Is it OK?". In a more informal tone sometimes the particle <i>-no</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">の</span></span></span>) is added instead to show a personal interest of the speaker: <i>Dōshite konai-no?</i> "Why aren't (you) coming?". Some simple queries are formed simply by mentioning the topic with an interrogative intonation to call for the hearer's attention: <i>Kore wa?</i> "(What about) this?"; <i>O-namae wa?</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">お名前は？</span></span></span>) "(What's your) name?".
</p><p>Negatives are formed by inflecting the verb. For example, <i>Pan o taberu</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">パンを食べる。</span></span></span>) "I will eat bread" or "I eat bread" becomes <i>Pan o tabenai</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">パンを食べない。</span></span></span>) "I will not eat bread" or "I do not eat bread". Plain negative forms are <i>i</i>-adjectives (see below) and inflect as such, e.g. <i>Pan o tabenakatta</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">パンを食べなかった。</span></span></span>) "I did not eat bread".
</p><p>The so-called <i>-te</i> verb form is used for a variety of purposes: either progressive or perfect aspect (see above); combining verbs in a temporal sequence (<i>Asagohan o tabete sugu dekakeru</i> "I'll eat breakfast and leave at once"), simple commands, conditional statements and permissions (<i>Dekakete-mo ii?</i> "May I go out?"), etc.
</p><p>The word <i>da</i> (plain), <i>desu</i> (polite) is the <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)#Japanese" title="Copula (linguistics)">copula</a> verb. It corresponds approximately to the English <i>be</i>, but often takes on other roles, including a marker for tense, when the verb is conjugated into its past form <i>datta</i> (plain), <i>deshita</i> (polite). This comes into use because only <i>i</i>-adjectives and verbs can carry tense in Japanese. Two additional common verbs are used to indicate existence ("there is") or, in some contexts, property: <i>aru</i> (negative <i>nai</i>) and <i>iru</i> (negative <i>inai</i>), for inanimate and animate things, respectively. For example, <i>Neko ga iru</i> "There's a cat", <i>Ii kangae-ga nai</i> "[I] haven't got a good idea".
</p><p>The verb "to do" (<i>suru</i>, polite form <i>shimasu</i>) is often used to make verbs from nouns (<i>ryōri suru</i> "to cook", <i>benkyō suru</i> "to study", etc.) and has been productive in creating modern slang words. Japanese also has a huge number of compound verbs to express concepts that are described in English using a verb and an adverbial particle (e.g. <i>tobidasu</i> "to fly out, to flee," from <i>tobu</i> "to fly, to jump" + <i>dasu</i> "to put out, to emit").
</p><p>There are three types of adjectives (see <a href="/wiki/Japanese_adjectives" title="Japanese adjectives">Japanese adjectives</a>):
</p>
<ol><li><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">形容詞</span></span></span> <i>keiyōshi</i>, or <i>i</i> adjectives, which have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_verb_conjugations" title="Japanese verb conjugations">conjugating</a> ending <i>i</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">い</span></span></span>) (such as <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">暑い</span></span></span> <i>atsui</i> "to be hot") which can become past (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">暑かった</span></span></span> <i>atsukatta</i> "it was hot"), or negative (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">暑くない</span></span></span> <i>atsuku nai</i> "it is not hot"). Note that <i>nai</i> is also an <i>i</i> adjective, which can become past (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">暑くなかった</span></span></span> <i>atsuku nakatta</i> "it was not hot").
<dl><dd><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">暑い日</span></span></span> <i>atsui hi</i> "a hot day".</dd></dl></li>
<li><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">形容動詞</span></span></span> <i>keiyōdōshi</i>, or <i>na</i> adjectives, which are followed by a form of the <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">copula</a>, usually <i>na</i>. For example, <i>hen</i> (strange)
<dl><dd><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">変な人</span></span></span> <i>hen na hito</i> "a strange person".</dd></dl></li>
<li><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">連体詞</span></span></span> <i>rentaishi</i>, also called true adjectives, such as <i>ano</i> "that"
<dl><dd><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">あの山</span></span></span> <i>ano yama</i> "that mountain".</dd></dl></li></ol>
<p>Both <i>keiyōshi</i> and <i>keiyōdōshi</i> may <a href="/wiki/Predicate_(grammar)" title="Predicate (grammar)">predicate</a> sentences. For example,
</p>
<blockquote><div class="poem">
<p><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ご飯が熱い。</span></span></span> <i>Gohan ga atsui.</i> "The rice is hot."<br/>
<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">彼は変だ。</span></span></span> <i>Kare wa hen da.</i> "He's strange."
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<p>Both inflect, though they do not show the full range of conjugation found in true verbs.
The <i>rentaishi</i> in Modern Japanese are few in number, and unlike the other words, are limited to directly modifying nouns. They never predicate sentences. Examples include <i>ookina</i> "big", <i>kono</i> "this", <i>iwayuru</i> "so-called" and <i>taishita</i> "amazing".
</p><p>Both <i>keiyōdōshi</i> and <i>keiyōshi</i> form <a href="/wiki/Adverb" title="Adverb">adverbs</a>, by following with <i>ni</i> in the case of <i>keiyōdōshi</i>:
</p>
<blockquote><p><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">変になる</span></span></span> <i>hen ni naru</i> "become strange",</p></blockquote>
<p>and by changing <i>i</i> to <i>ku</i> in the case of <i>keiyōshi</i>:
</p>
<blockquote><p><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">熱くなる</span></span></span> <i>atsuku naru</i> "become hot".</p></blockquote>
<p>The grammatical function of nouns is indicated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postposition" title="Postposition">postpositions</a>, also called <a href="/wiki/Japanese_particles" title="Japanese particles">particles</a>. These include for example:
</p>
<ul><li><b><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">が</span></span></span> <i>ga</i></b> for the <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative case</a>.</li></ul>
<dl><dd><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><b>彼が</b>やった。</span></span></span><i><b>Kare ga</b> yatta.</i> "He did it."</dd></dl>
<ul><li><b><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">に</span></span></span> <i>ni</i></b> for the <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative case</a>.</li></ul>
<dl><dd><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><b>田中さんに</b>あげて下さい。</span></span></span> <i><b>Tanaka-san ni</b> agete kudasai</i> "Please give it <b>to Mx Tanaka</b>."</dd></dl>
<p>It is also used for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lative" title="Lative">lative</a> case, indicating a motion to a location.
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><b>日本に</b>行きたい。</span></span></span> <i><b>Nihon ni</b> ikitai</i> "I want to go <b>to Japan</b>."</dd></dl>
<ul><li>However, <b>へ <i>e</i></b> is more commonly used for the lative case.</li></ul>
<dl><dd><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><b>パーティーへ</b>行かないか。</span></span></span> <i><b>pātī e</b> ikanai ka?</i> "Won't you go <b>to the party</b>?"</dd></dl>
<ul><li><b><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">の</span></span></span> <i>no</i></b> for the <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive case</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> or nominalizing phrases.</li></ul>
<dl><dd><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><b>私の</b>カメラ。</span></span></span> <b>watashi no</b> kamera<i> "<b>my</b> camera"</i></dd>
<dd><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">スキーに行く<b>の</b>が好きです。</span></span></span> <i>Sukī-ni iku <b>no</b> ga suki desu</i> "(I) like go<b>ing</b> skiing."</dd></dl>
<ul><li><b><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">を</span></span></span> <i>o</i></b> for the <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative case</a>.</li></ul>
<dl><dd><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><b>何を</b>食べますか。</span></span></span> <i><b>Nani o</b> tabemasu ka?</i> "<b>What</b> will (you) eat?"</dd></dl>
<ul><li><b><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">は</span></span></span> <i>wa</i></b> for the topic. It can co-exist with the case markers listed above, and it overrides <i>ga</i> and (in most cases) <i>o</i>.</li></ul>
<dl><dd><span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><b>私は</b>寿司がいいです。</span></span></span> <i><b>Watashi wa</b> sushi ga ii desu.</i> (literally) "<b>As for me</b>, sushi is good." The nominative marker <i>ga</i> after <i>watashi</i> is hidden under <i>wa</i>.</dd></dl>
<p>Note: The subtle difference between <i><b>wa</b></i> and <i><b>ga</b></i> in Japanese cannot be derived from the English language as such, because the distinction between sentence topic and subject is not made there. While <i>wa</i> indicates the topic, which the rest of the sentence describes or acts upon, it carries the implication that the subject indicated by <i>wa</i> is not unique, or may be part of a larger group.
</p>
<blockquote><p><i>Ikeda-san <b>wa</b> yonjū-ni sai da.</i> "As for Mx Ikeda, they are forty-two years old." Others in the group may also be of that age.</p></blockquote>
<p>Absence of <i>wa</i> often means the subject is the <a href="/wiki/Focus_(linguistics)" title="Focus (linguistics)">focus</a> of the sentence.
</p>
<blockquote><p><i>Ikeda-san <b>ga</b> yonjū-ni sai da.</i> "It is Mx Ikeda who is forty-two years old." This is a reply to an implicit or explicit question, such as "who in this group is forty-two years old?"</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politeness">Politeness</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Honorific_speech_in_Japanese" title="Honorific speech in Japanese">Honorific speech in Japanese</a></div>
<p>Japanese has an extensive grammatical system to express politeness and formality. This reflects the hierarchical nature of Japanese society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Japanese language can express differing levels in social status. The differences in social position are determined by a variety of factors including job, age, experience, or even psychological state (e.g., a person asking a favour tends to do so politely). The person in the lower position is expected to use a polite form of speech, whereas the other person might use a plainer form. Strangers will also speak to each other politely. Japanese children rarely use polite speech until they are teens, at which point they are expected to begin speaking in a more adult manner. <i>See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uchi-soto" title="Uchi-soto">uchi-soto</a></i>.
</p><p>Whereas <i>teineigo</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">丁寧語</span></span></span>) (polite language) is commonly an <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflectional</a> system, <i>sonkeigo</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">尊敬語</span></span></span>) (respectful language) and <i>kenjōgo</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">謙譲語</span></span></span>) (humble language) often employ many special honorific and humble alternate verbs: <i>iku</i> "go" becomes <i>ikimasu</i> in polite form, but is replaced by <i>irassharu</i> in honorific speech and <i>ukagau</i> or <i>mairu</i> in humble speech.
</p><p>The difference between honorific and humble speech is particularly pronounced in the Japanese language. Humble language is used to talk about oneself or one's own group (company, family) whilst honorific language is mostly used when describing the interlocutor and their group. For example, the <i>-san</i> suffix ("Mr" "Mrs.", "Miss", or "Mx") is an example of honorific language. It is not used to talk about oneself or when talking about someone from one's company to an external person, since the company is the speaker's in-group. When speaking directly to one's superior in one's company or when speaking with other employees within one's company about a superior, a Japanese person will use vocabulary and inflections of the honorific register to refer to the in-group superior and their speech and actions. When speaking to a person from another company (i.e., a member of an out-group), however, a Japanese person will use the plain or the humble register to refer to the speech and actions of their own in-group superiors. In short, the register used in Japanese to refer to the person, speech, or actions of any particular individual varies depending on the relationship (either in-group or out-group) between the speaker and listener, as well as depending on the relative status of the speaker, listener, and third-person referents.
</p><p>Most <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a> in the Japanese language may be made polite by the addition of <i>o-</i> or <i>go-</i> as a prefix. <i>o-</i> is generally used for words of native Japanese origin, whereas <i>go-</i> is affixed to words of Chinese derivation. In some cases, the prefix has become a fixed part of the word, and is included even in regular speech, such as <i>gohan</i> 'cooked rice; meal.' Such a construction often indicates deference to either the item's owner or to the object itself. For example, the word <i>tomodachi</i> 'friend,' would become <i>o-tomodachi</i> when referring to the friend of someone of higher status (though mothers often use this form to refer to their children's friends). On the other hand, a polite speaker may sometimes refer to <i>mizu</i> 'water' as <i>o-mizu</i> in order to show politeness.
</p><p>Most Japanese people employ politeness to indicate a lack of familiarity. That is, they use polite forms for new acquaintances, but if a relationship becomes more intimate, they no longer use them. This occurs regardless of age, social class, or gender.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yamato_kotoba" title="Yamato kotoba">Yamato kotoba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sino-Japanese_vocabulary" title="Sino-Japanese vocabulary">Sino-Japanese vocabulary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gairaigo" title="Gairaigo">Gairaigo</a></div>
<p>There are three main sources of words in the Japanese language, the <i>yamato kotoba</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">大和言葉</span></span></span>) or <i>wago</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">和語</span></span></span>), <i>kango</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">漢語</span></span></span>), and <i>gairaigo</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">外来語</span></span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The original language of Japan, or at least the original language of a certain population that was ancestral to a significant portion of the historical and present Japanese nation, was the so-called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yamato_kotoba" title="Yamato kotoba">yamato kotoba</a></i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">大和言葉</span></span></span> or infrequently <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">大和詞</span></span></span>, i.e. "<a href="/wiki/Yamato_people" title="Yamato people">Yamato</a> words"), which in scholarly contexts is sometimes referred to as <i>wago</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">和語</span></span></span> or rarely <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">倭語</span></span></span>, i.e. the "<a href="/wiki/Wa_(Japan)" title="Wa (Japan)">Wa</a> language"). In addition to words from this original language, present-day Japanese includes a number of words that were either borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> or constructed from Chinese roots following Chinese patterns. These words, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Sino-Japanese_vocabulary" title="Sino-Japanese vocabulary">kango</a></i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">漢語</span></span></span>), entered the language from the 5th century onwards via contact with Chinese culture. According to the <i>Shinsen Kokugo Jiten</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">新選国語辞典</span></span>)</span> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_dictionary" title="Japanese dictionary">Japanese dictionary</a>, <i>kango</i> comprise 49.1% of the total vocabulary, <i>wago</i> make up 33.8%, other foreign words or <i><a href="/wiki/Gairaigo" title="Gairaigo">gairaigo</a></i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">外来語</span></span></span>) account for 8.8%, and the remaining 8.3% constitute hybridized words or <i>konshugo</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">混種語</span></span></span>) that draw elements from more than one language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are also a great number of words of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mimetic" title="Mimetic">mimetic</a> origin in Japanese, with Japanese having a rich collection of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_sound_symbolism" title="Japanese sound symbolism">sound symbolism</a>, both <a href="/wiki/Onomatopoeia" title="Onomatopoeia">onomatopoeia</a> for physical sounds, and more <a href="/wiki/Abstraction" title="Abstraction">abstract</a> words. A small number of words have come into Japanese from the <a href="/wiki/Ainu_language" title="Ainu language">Ainu language</a>. <i>Tonakai</i> (<a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a>), <i>rakko</i> (<a href="/wiki/Sea_otter" title="Sea otter">sea otter</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Shishamo" title="Shishamo">shishamo</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smelts" title="Smelts">smelt</a>, a type of fish) are well-known examples of words of Ainu origin.
</p><p>Words of different origins occupy different <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">registers</a> in Japanese. Like Latin-derived words in English, <i>kango</i> words are typically perceived as somewhat formal or academic compared to equivalent Yamato words. Indeed, it is generally fair to say that an English word derived from Latin/French roots typically corresponds to a Sino-Japanese word in Japanese, whereas an <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Anglo-Saxon word</a> would best be translated by a Yamato equivalent.
</p><p>Incorporating vocabulary from <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">European languages</a>, <i>gairaigo</i>, began with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_words_of_Portuguese_origin" title="Japanese words of Portuguese origin">borrowings from Portuguese</a> in the 16th century, followed by words from <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> during Japan's <a href="/wiki/Sakoku" title="Sakoku">long isolation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a>. With the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> and the reopening of Japan in the 19th century, borrowing occurred from <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, and <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>. Today most borrowings are from English.
</p><p>In the Meiji era, the Japanese also coined many neologisms using Chinese roots and morphology to translate European concepts;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> these are known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wasei_kango" title="Wasei kango">wasei kango</a> (Japanese-made Chinese words). Many of these were then imported into Chinese, Korean, and Vietnamese via their kanji in the late 19th and early 20th centuries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> For example, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">seiji</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">政治</span></span>, "politics")</span>, and <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">kagaku</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">化学</span></span>, "chemistry")</span> are words derived from Chinese roots that were first created and used by the Japanese, and only later borrowed into Chinese and other East Asian languages. As a result, Japanese, Chinese, Korean, and Vietnamese share a large common corpus of vocabulary in the same way many Greek- and Latin-derived words – both inherited or borrowed into European languages, or modern coinages from Greek or Latin roots – are shared among modern European languages – see <a href="/wiki/Classical_compound" title="Classical compound">classical compound</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the past few decades, <i><a href="/wiki/Wasei-eigo" title="Wasei-eigo">wasei-eigo</a></i> ("made-in-Japan English") has become a prominent phenomenon. Words such as <i>wanpatān</i> <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ワンパターン</span></span></span> (&lt; <i>one</i> + <i>pattern</i>, "to be in a rut", "to have a one-track mind") and <i>sukinshippu</i> <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">スキンシップ</span></span></span> (&lt; <i>skin</i> + <i>-ship</i>, "physical contact"), although coined by compounding English roots, are nonsensical in most non-Japanese contexts; exceptions exist in nearby languages such as Korean however, which often use words such as <i>skinship</i> and <i>rimokon</i> (remote control) in the same way as in Japanese.
</p><p>The popularity of many Japanese cultural exports has made some native Japanese words familiar in English, including <i><a href="/wiki/Emoji" title="Emoji">emoji</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Futon" title="Futon">futon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haiku" title="Haiku">haiku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judo" title="Judo">judo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kamikaze" title="Kamikaze">kamikaze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karaoke" title="Karaoke">karaoke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karate" title="Karate">karate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ninja" title="Ninja">ninja</a>, <a href="/wiki/Origami" title="Origami">origami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pulled_rickshaw" title="Pulled rickshaw">rickshaw</a></i> (from <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">人力車</span></span></span> <i>jinrikisha</i>), <i><a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">samurai</a>, <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Sayonara_(disambiguation)" title="Sayonara (disambiguation)">sayonara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudoku" title="Sudoku">Sudoku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sumo" title="Sumo">sumo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sushi" title="Sushi">sushi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tycoon" title="Tycoon">tycoon</a></i>. See <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_words_of_Japanese_origin" title="List of English words of Japanese origin">list of English words of Japanese origin</a> for more.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">Writing system</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_writing_system" title="Japanese writing system">Japanese writing system</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_braille" title="Japanese braille">Japanese braille</a></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_2">History</span></h3>
<p>Literacy was introduced to Japan in the form of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_writing_system" title="Chinese writing system">Chinese writing system</a>, by way of <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a> before the 5th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Using this language, the Japanese king <a href="/wiki/Five_kings_of_Wa" title="Five kings of Wa">Bu</a> presented a petition to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Shun_of_Liu_Song" title="Emperor Shun of Liu Song">Emperor Shun of Liu Song</a> in AD 478.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[a]</a></sup> After the ruin of Baekje, Japan invited scholars from China to learn more of the Chinese writing system. Japanese emperors gave an official rank to Chinese scholars (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">続守言/薩弘恪</span></span>/<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[c]</a></sup><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">袁晋卿</span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[d]</a></sup>) and spread the use of Chinese characters from the 7th century to the 8th century.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nihongo_ichiran_01-converted.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1532" data-file-width="1356" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nihongo_ichiran_01-converted.svg/220px-Nihongo_ichiran_01-converted.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nihongo_ichiran_01-converted.svg/330px-Nihongo_ichiran_01-converted.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nihongo_ichiran_01-converted.svg/440px-Nihongo_ichiran_01-converted.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nihongo_ichiran_01-converted.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Table of Kana (including <a href="/wiki/Y%C5%8Don" title="Yōon">Youon</a>): <a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">Hiragana</a> top, <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">Katakana</a> in the center and Romanized equivalents at the bottom</div></div></div>
<p>At first, the Japanese wrote in <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a>, with Japanese names represented by characters used for their meanings and not their sounds. Later, during the 7th century AD, the Chinese-sounding phoneme principle was used to write pure Japanese poetry and prose, but some Japanese words were still written with characters for their meaning and not the original Chinese sound. This is when the history of Japanese as a written language begins in its own right. By this time, the Japanese language was already very distinct from the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_languages" title="Ryukyuan languages">Ryukyuan languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>An example of this mixed style is the <a href="/wiki/Kojiki" title="Kojiki">Kojiki</a>, which was written in AD 712. Japanese writers then started to use Chinese characters to write Japanese in a style known as <i>man'yōgana</i>, a syllabic script which used Chinese characters for their sounds in order to transcribe the words of Japanese speech syllable by syllable.
</p><p>Over time, a writing system evolved. <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a>) were used to write either words borrowed from Chinese, or Japanese words with the same or similar meanings. Chinese characters were also used to write grammatical elements, were simplified, and eventually became two syllabic scripts: <a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a> which were developed based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manyogana" title="Manyogana">Manyogana</a>. Some scholars claim that Manyogana originated from Baekje, but this hypothesis is denied by mainstream Japanese scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yoshinori Kobayashi and Alexander Vovin argued that Japan's Katakana originated from the <a href="/wiki/Gugyeol" title="Gugyeol">Gugyeol</a> writing system used during the <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a> Dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-55">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hiragana and katakana were first simplified from kanji, and hiragana, emerging somewhere around the 9th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup> was mainly used by women. Hiragana was seen as an informal language, whereas katakana and kanji were considered more formal and was typically used by men and in official settings. However, because of hiragana's accessibility, more and more people began using it. Eventually, by the 10th century, hiragana was used by everyone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern Japanese is written in a mixture of three main systems: <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a>, characters of Chinese origin used to represent both Chinese <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a> into Japanese and a number of native Japanese <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a>; and two <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabaries</a>: <a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> (or romaji in Japanese) is used to a certain extent, such as for imported acronyms and to transcribe Japanese names and in other instances where non-Japanese speakers need to know how to pronounce a word (such as "ramen" at a restaurant). Arabic numerals are much more common than the kanji when used in counting, but kanji numerals are still used in compounds, such as <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">統一</span></span></span> <i>tōitsu</i> ("unification").
</p><p>Historically, attempts to limit the number of kanji in use commenced in the mid-19th century, but did not become a matter of government intervention until after Japan's defeat in the Second World War. During the period of post-war occupation (and influenced by the views of some U.S. officials), various schemes including the complete abolition of kanji and exclusive use of rōmaji were considered. The <i><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">jōyō kanji</a></i> ("common use kanji", originally called <i><a href="/wiki/T%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Tōyō kanji">tōyō kanji</a></i> [kanji for general use]) scheme arose as a compromise solution.
</p><p>Japanese students begin to learn kanji from their first year at elementary school. A guideline created by the Japanese Ministry of Education, the list of <i><a href="/wiki/Ky%C5%8Diku_kanji" title="Kyōiku kanji">kyōiku kanji</a></i> ("education kanji", a subset of <i><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">jōyō kanji</a></i>), specifies the 1,006 simple characters a child is to learn by the end of sixth grade. Children continue to study another 1,130 characters in junior high school, covering in total 2,136 <i><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">jōyō kanji</a></i>. The official list of <i><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">jōyō kanji</a></i> was revised several times, but the total number of officially sanctioned characters remained largely unchanged.
</p><p>As for kanji for personal names, the circumstances are somewhat complicated. <i><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">Jōyō kanji</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Jinmeiy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jinmeiyō kanji">jinmeiyō kanji</a></i> (an appendix of additional characters for names) are approved for registering personal names. Names containing unapproved characters are denied registration. However, as with the list of <i><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">jōyō kanji</a></i>, criteria for inclusion were often arbitrary and led to many common and popular characters being disapproved for use. Under popular pressure and following a court decision holding the exclusion of common characters unlawful, the list of <i><a href="/wiki/Jinmeiy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jinmeiyō kanji">jinmeiyō kanji</a></i> was substantially extended from 92 in 1951 (the year it was first decreed) to 983 in 2004. Furthermore, families whose names are not on these lists were permitted to continue using the older forms.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hiragana">Hiragana</span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">Hiragana</a></i> are used for words without kanji representation, for words no longer written in kanji, for replacement of rare kanji that may be unfamiliar to intended readers, and also following kanji to show conjugational endings. Because of the way verbs (and adjectives) in Japanese are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_verb_conjugations" title="Japanese verb conjugations">conjugated</a>, kanji alone cannot fully convey Japanese tense and mood, as kanji cannot be subject to variation when written without losing their meaning. For this reason, hiragana are appended to kanji to show verb and adjective conjugations. Hiragana used in this way are called <a href="/wiki/Okurigana" title="Okurigana">okurigana</a>. Hiragana can also be written in a superscript called <a href="/wiki/Furigana" title="Furigana">furigana</a> above or beside a kanji to show the proper reading. This is done to facilitate learning, as well as to clarify particularly old or obscure (or sometimes invented) readings.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Katakana">Katakana</span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">Katakana</a></i>, like hiragana, constitute a <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabary</a>; katakana are primarily used to write foreign words, plant and animal names, and for emphasis. For example, "Australia" has been adapted as <i>Ōsutoraria</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">オーストラリア</span></span></span>), and "supermarket" has been adapted and shortened into <i>sūpā</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">スーパー</span></span></span>).
</p><p>Alexander Vovin argued that Japan's katakana originated from the <a href="/wiki/Gugyeol" title="Gugyeol">Gugyeol</a> writing system used during the <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a> Dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-55">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yoshinori Kobayashi of Hiroshima University asserted the hypothesis that katakana originated from <a href="/wiki/Gugyeol" title="Gugyeol">Gugyeol</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-native_study">Non-native study</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language_education" title="Japanese language education">Japanese language education</a></div>
<p>Many major universities throughout the world provide Japanese language courses, and a number of secondary and even primary schools worldwide offer courses in the language. This is a significant increase from before <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>; in 1940, only 65 Americans not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_American" title="Japanese American">of Japanese descent</a> were able to read, write and understand the language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>International interest in the Japanese language dates from the 19th century but has become more prevalent following Japan's economic bubble of the 1980s and the global popularity of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_popular_culture" title="Japanese popular culture">Japanese popular culture</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a> and <a href="/wiki/Video_game" title="Video game">video games</a>) since the 1990s. As of 2015, more than 3.6 million people studied the language worldwide, primarily in East and Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japanese_as_Foreign_Language_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Japanese_as_Foreign_Language-59">[55]</a></sup> Nearly one million Chinese, 745,000 Indonesians, 556,000 South Koreans and 357,000 Australians studied Japanese in lower and higher educational institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japanese_as_Foreign_Language_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Japanese_as_Foreign_Language-59">[55]</a></sup> Between 2012 and 2015, considerable growth of learners originated in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> (20.5%), <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> (34.1%), <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> (38.7%) and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> (54.4%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japanese_as_Foreign_Language_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Japanese_as_Foreign_Language-59">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Japanese government provides standardized tests to measure spoken and written comprehension of Japanese for second language learners; the most prominent is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Language_Proficiency_Test" title="Japanese Language Proficiency Test">Japanese Language Proficiency Test</a> (JLPT), which features five levels of exams. The JLPT is offered twice a year.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Example_text">Example text</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><audio class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="20" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights-jpn-Art1.ogg" id="mwe_player_1" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="96056" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (96 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6e/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights-jpn-Art1.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="160520" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/6e/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights-jpn-Art1.ogg/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights-jpn-Art1.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights-jpn-Art1.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Recording of the first article of the UN Universal Declaration of Human Rights in Japanese.</div></div></div>
<p>Article 1 of the <i><a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a></i> in Japanese:
</p>
<dl><dd><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">すべての人間は、生まれながらにして自由であり、かつ、尊厳と権利と について平等である。人間は、理性と良心とを授けられており、互いに同胞の精神をもって行動しなければならない。</span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[56]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Japanese" title="Romanization of Japanese">transcription</a> of the example text into <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Subete no ningen wa, umarenagara ni shite jiyū de ari, katsu, songen to kenri to ni tsuite byōdō de aru. Ningen wa, risei to ryōshin to o sazukerarete ori, tagai ni dōhō no seishin o motte kōdō shinakereba naranai.</i></span></dd></dl>
<p>Article 1 of the <i>Universal Declaration of Human Rights</i> in English:
</p>
<dl><dd><i>All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights. They are endowed with reason and conscience and should act towards one another in a spirit of brotherhood.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[57]</a></sup></dd></dl>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Song" title="Book of Song">Book of Song</a> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">順帝昇明二年，倭王武遣使上表曰：封國偏遠，作藩于外，自昔祖禰，躬擐甲冑，跋渉山川，不遑寧處。東征毛人五十國，西服衆夷六十六國，渡平海北九十五國，王道融泰，廓土遐畿，累葉朝宗，不愆于歳。臣雖下愚，忝胤先緒，驅率所統，歸崇天極，道逕百濟，裝治船舫，而句驪無道，圖欲見吞，掠抄邊隸，虔劉不已，毎致稽滯，以失良風。雖曰進路，或通或不。臣亡考濟實忿寇讎，壅塞天路，控弦百萬，義聲感激，方欲大舉，奄喪父兄，使垂成之功，不獲一簣。居在諒闇，不動兵甲，是以偃息未捷。至今欲練甲治兵，申父兄之志，義士虎賁，文武效功，白刃交前，亦所不顧。若以帝德覆載，摧此強敵，克靖方難，無替前功。竊自假開府儀同三司，其餘咸各假授，以勸忠節。詔除武使持節督倭、新羅、任那、加羅、秦韓六國諸軍事、安東大將軍、倭國王。至齊建元中，及梁武帝時，并來朝貢。</span></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nihon_shoki" title="Nihon shoki">Nihon shoki</a> Chapter 30:<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">持統五年 九月己巳朔壬申。賜音博士大唐続守言。薩弘恪。書博士百済末士善信、銀人二十両。</span></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nihon_shoki" title="Nihon shoki">Nihon shoki</a> Chapter 30:<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">持統六年 十二月辛酉朔甲戌。賜音博士続守言。薩弘恪水田人四町</span></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Shoku_Nihongi" title="Shoku Nihongi">Shoku Nihongi</a> <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">宝亀九年 十二月庚寅。玄蕃頭従五位上袁晋卿賜姓清村宿禰。晋卿唐人也。天平七年随我朝使帰朝。時年十八九。学得文選爾雅音。為大学音博士。於後。歴大学頭安房守。</span></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHall2013">Hall, Kathleen Currie (2013). <a class="external text" href="http://homes.chass.utoronto.ca/~cla-acl/actes2013/Hall-2013.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Documenting phonological change: A comparison of two Japanese phonemic splits"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>.  In Luo, Shan (ed.). <i>Proceedings of the 2013 Annual Conference of the Canadian Linguistic Association</i>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20191212034227/http://homes.chass.utoronto.ca/~cla-acl/actes2013/Hall-2013.pdf" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span> from the original on 2019-12-12<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2019-06-01</span></span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Documenting+phonological+change%3A+A+comparison+of+two+Japanese+phonemic+splits&amp;rft.btitle=Proceedings+of+the+2013+Annual+Conference+of+the+Canadian+Linguistic+Association&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.aulast=Hall&amp;rft.aufirst=Kathleen+Currie&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fhomes.chass.utoronto.ca%2F~cla-acl%2Factes2013%2FHall-2013.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJapanese+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Japanese is listed as one of the official languages of <a href="/wiki/Angaur" title="Angaur">Angaur</a> state, <a href="/wiki/Palau" title="Palau">Palau</a> (<a class="external text" href="http://www.ethnologue.com/show_country.asp?name=PW" rel="nofollow">Ethnologe</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20071001180034/http://www.ethnologue.com/show_country.asp?name=PW" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2007-10-01 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://www.cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/palau/" rel="nofollow">CIA World Factbook</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210203032225/https://www.cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/palau/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2021-02-03 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>). However, very few Japanese speakers were recorded in the <a class="external text" href="http://www.spc.int/prism/country/pw/stats/PalauStats/Publication/2005CENSUS.pdf" rel="nofollow">2005 census</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080216045724/http://www.spc.int/prism/country/pw/stats/PalauStats/Publication/2005CENSUS.pdf" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2008-02-16 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>.</span>
</li>
